<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Buchanan_sculpture_at_National_Portrait_Gallery_IMG_4538.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/James_Buchanan_sculpture_at_National_Portrait_Gallery_IMG_4538.JPG/220px-James_Buchanan_sculpture_at_National_Portrait_Gallery_IMG_4538.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/James_Buchanan_sculpture_at_National_Portrait_Gallery_IMG_4538.JPG/330px-James_Buchanan_sculpture_at_National_Portrait_Gallery_IMG_4538.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/James_Buchanan_sculpture_at_National_Portrait_Gallery_IMG_4538.JPG/440px-James_Buchanan_sculpture_at_National_Portrait_Gallery_IMG_4538.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

<p><b>James Buchanan, Jr.</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ju?/ long 'u' in 'cute'">ju?</span><span title="/?/ primary stress follows">?</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/?n/ 'on' in 'button'">?n</span></span>/</a></span></span>; April 23, 1791&#160;– June 1, 1868) was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">15th</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> (1857–1861), serving immediately prior to the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. He represented <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> and later the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a>, then served as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_Russia" title="United States Ambassador to Russia">Minister to Russia</a> under President <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>. He was named <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> under President <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a>, and is to date the last former Secretary of State to serve as President of the United States. After Buchanan turned down an offer to sit on the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a>, President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a> appointed him <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_the_United_Kingdom" title="United States Ambassador to the United Kingdom">Ambassador to the United Kingdom</a>, in which capacity he helped draft the <a href="/wiki/Ostend_Manifesto" title="Ostend Manifesto">Ostend Manifesto</a>.</p>
<p>Buchanan was nominated by the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1856" title="United States presidential election, 1856">1856 presidential election</a>. Throughout most of Pierce's term, he had been stationed in London as a minister to the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_St._James%27s" title="Court of St. James's" class="mw-redirect">Court of St. James's</a>, so was not caught up in the crossfire of sectional politics that dominated the country. His subsequent election victory took place in a three-man race with <a href="/wiki/John_C._Fr%C3%A9mont" title="John C. Frémont">John C. Frémont</a> and <a href="/wiki/Millard_Fillmore" title="Millard Fillmore">Millard Fillmore</a>. As President, he was often called a "<a href="/wiki/Doughface" title="Doughface">doughface</a>", a Northerner with Southern sympathies, who battled with <a href="/wiki/Stephen_A._Douglas" title="Stephen A. Douglas">Stephen A. Douglas</a> for control of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Democratic_Party" title="History of the United States Democratic Party">Democratic Party</a>. Buchanan's efforts to <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_the_American_Civil_War" title="Origins of the American Civil War">maintain peace</a> between the North and the South alienated both sides, and the Southern states declared their <a href="/wiki/Secession_in_the_United_States" title="Secession in the United States">secession</a> in the prologue to the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. Buchanan's view of record was that secession was illegal, but that going to war to stop it was also illegal. Buchanan, an attorney, was noted for his mantra, "I acknowledge no master but the law."<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By the time he left office, popular opinion was against him, and the Democratic Party had split. Buchanan had once aspired to be a president that would rank in history with <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> However, his inability to identify a ground for peace or address the sharply divided proslavery and antislavery partisans with a unifying principle on the brink of the Civil War has led to his consistent ranking by historians as one of the <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_United_States_Presidents" title="Historical rankings of United States Presidents" class="mw-redirect">worst presidents</a> in American history. Historians in both 2006 and 2009 voted his failure to deal with secession the worst presidential mistake ever made.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He is, to date, the only president from <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> and the only president to remain a lifelong <a href="/wiki/Bachelor" title="Bachelor">bachelor</a>. He is also the last president born in the 18th century.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buchanan_Home_Mercersburg.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Buchanan_Home_Mercersburg.jpg/220px-Buchanan_Home_Mercersburg.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Buchanan_Home_Mercersburg.jpg/330px-Buchanan_Home_Mercersburg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Buchanan_Home_Mercersburg.jpg/440px-Buchanan_Home_Mercersburg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4938" data-file-height="3296" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buchanan_Home_Mercersburg.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Buchanan's birthplace relocated log cabin in Mercersburg, PA</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Buchanan was born in a <a href="/wiki/Log_cabin" title="Log cabin">log cabin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cove_Gap,_Pennsylvania" title="Cove Gap, Pennsylvania">Cove Gap, Pennsylvania</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Buchanan%27s_Birthplace_State_Park" title="Buchanan's Birthplace State Park">Buchanan's Birthplace State Park</a>), in <a href="/wiki/Franklin_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Franklin County, Pennsylvania">Franklin County</a>, on April 23, 1791, to James Buchanan, Sr. (1761–1821), a businessman, merchant, and farmer, and Elizabeth Speer, an educated woman (1767–1833).<sup id="cite_ref-ancestry_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ancestry-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> His parents were both of <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_people" title="Ulster Scots people">Ulster Scots</a> descent, the father having emigrated from <a href="/wiki/Donegal" title="Donegal">Donegal</a>, Ireland, in 1783. Buchanan had six sisters and four brothers.<sup id="cite_ref-ancestry_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ancestry-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1797, the family moved to nearby <a href="/wiki/Mercersburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Mercersburg, Pennsylvania">Mercersburg, Pennsylvania</a>. The home in Mercersburg was later turned into the <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan_Hotel" title="James Buchanan Hotel" class="mw-redirect">James Buchanan Hotel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Buchanan attended the village academy (Old Stone Academy) and later <a href="/wiki/Dickinson_College" title="Dickinson College">Dickinson College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Carlisle,_Pennsylvania" title="Carlisle, Pennsylvania">Carlisle, Pennsylvania</a>. Though he was nearly expelled at one point for poor behavior, he pleaded for a second chance and subsequently graduated with honors on September 19, 1809.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Later that year, he moved to <a href="/wiki/Lancaster,_Pennsylvania" title="Lancaster, Pennsylvania">Lancaster</a>, where he studied law and was admitted to the bar in 1812.</p>
<p>A dedicated <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist</a>, he initially opposed the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> because he believed it was an unnecessary conflict. When the British invaded neighboring <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, he joined a volunteer light <a href="/wiki/Dragoon#United_States" title="Dragoon">dragoon unit</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Private_(rank)" title="Private (rank)">private</a> and served in the defense of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Baltimore" title="Battle of Baltimore">Baltimore</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Buchanan is the only president with military experience who did not, at some point, serve as an officer.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>An active <a href="/wiki/Freemason" title="Freemason" class="mw-redirect">Freemason</a>, he was the Master of <a href="/wiki/Masonic_Lodge" title="Masonic Lodge">Masonic Lodge</a> No. 43 in Lancaster, Pennsylvania, and a District Deputy <a href="/wiki/Master_Mason" title="Master Mason" class="mw-redirect">Grand Master</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Lodge_of_Pennsylvania" title="Grand Lodge of Pennsylvania">Grand Lodge of Pennsylvania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_career">Political career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Political career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Buchanan began his political career in the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_House_of_Representatives" title="Pennsylvania House of Representatives">Pennsylvania House of Representatives</a> from 1814 to 1816 as a member of the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party_(United_States)" title="Federalist Party (United States)" class="mw-redirect">Federalist Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> He was elected to the <a href="/wiki/17th_United_States_Congress" title="17th United States Congress">17th United States Congress</a> and to the four succeeding Congresses (March 4, 1821&#160;– March 4, 1831), serving as chairman of the <a href="/wiki/U.S._House_Committee_on_the_Judiciary" title="U.S. House Committee on the Judiciary" class="mw-redirect">U.S. House Committee on the Judiciary</a> in the <a href="/wiki/21st_United_States_Congress" title="21st United States Congress">21st United States Congress</a>. In 1830, he was among the members appointed by the House to conduct <a href="/wiki/Impeachment" title="Impeachment">impeachment</a> proceedings against <a href="/wiki/James_H._Peck" title="James H. Peck">James H. Peck</a>, judge of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_District_Court_for_the_District_of_Missouri" title="United States District Court for the District of Missouri" class="mw-redirect">United States District Court for the District of Missouri</a>. Peck was charged with abuse of the contempt power, but was ultimately acquitted.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Buchanan did not seek reelection, and from 1832 to 1833, he served as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_Russia" title="United States Ambassador to Russia">Minister to Russia</a>, appointed by <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>.</p>
<p>With the Federalist Party long defunct, Buchanan was elected as a Democrat to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senate</a> to fill a vacancy and served from December 1834; he was reelected in 1837 and 1843, and resigned in 1845 to accept nomination of him as Secretary of State by President <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a>. He was chairman of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Committee_on_Foreign_Relations" title="United States Senate Committee on Foreign Relations">Committee on Foreign Relations</a> from 1836 to 1841.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After the death of Supreme Court Justice <a href="/wiki/Henry_Baldwin_(judge)" title="Henry Baldwin (judge)">Henry Baldwin</a> in 1844, Polk nominated Buchanan to fill the vacancy in March 1845, but he declined that nomination because he felt compelled to complete his collaboration on the Oregon Treaty negotiations. The seat was eventually filled by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cooper_Grier" title="Robert Cooper Grier">Robert Cooper Grier</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Buchanan served as Secretary of State under Polk from 1845 to 1849, despite objections from Buchanan's rival, Vice President <a href="/wiki/George_M._Dallas" title="George M. Dallas">George Dallas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> In this capacity, he helped negotiate the 1846 <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Treaty" title="Oregon Treaty">Oregon Treaty</a> establishing the <a href="/wiki/49th_parallel_north" title="49th parallel north">49th parallel</a> as the northern boundary of the western United States.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> No Secretary of State has become President since Buchanan, although <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a>, the 27th President of the United States, often served as Acting Secretary of State during the <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> administration.</p>
<p>In 1852, Buchanan was named president of the Board of Trustees of <a href="/wiki/Franklin_and_Marshall_College" title="Franklin and Marshall College" class="mw-redirect">Franklin and Marshall College</a> in his hometown of Lancaster, Pennsylvania, and he served in this capacity until 1866,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> despite a false report that he was fired.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He served as minister to the Court of St. James's (Britain) from 1853 to 1856, during which time he helped to draft a memorandum that became known as the <a href="/wiki/Ostend_Manifesto" title="Ostend Manifesto">Ostend Manifesto</a>. He signed the memorandum along with <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Soul%C3%A9" title="Pierre Soulé">Pierre Soulé</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Y._Mason" title="John Y. Mason">John Mason</a>. This document proposed the purchase from Spain of <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, then in the midst of revolution and near bankruptcy, declaring the island "as necessary to the North American republic as any of its present ... family of states." Against Buchanan's recommendation, the final draft of the manifesto suggested that "wresting it from Spain" if Spain refused to sell would be justified "by every law, human and Divine".<sup id="cite_ref-battle_cry_of_freedom_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-battle_cry_of_freedom-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> The manifesto, generally considered a blunder overall, was never acted upon, but weakened the Pierce administration and support for <a href="/wiki/Manifest_Destiny" title="Manifest Destiny" class="mw-redirect">Manifest Destiny</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-battle_cry_of_freedom_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-battle_cry_of_freedom-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_election_of_1856">Presidential election of 1856</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Presidential election of 1856">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DemocraticPresidentialCounty1856.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/DemocraticPresidentialCounty1856.png/250px-DemocraticPresidentialCounty1856.png" width="250" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/DemocraticPresidentialCounty1856.png/375px-DemocraticPresidentialCounty1856.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/DemocraticPresidentialCounty1856.png/500px-DemocraticPresidentialCounty1856.png 2x" data-file-width="2800" data-file-height="2200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DemocraticPresidentialCounty1856.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Results by county explicitly indicating the percentage for Buchanan in each county</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1856" title="United States presidential election, 1856">United States presidential election, 1856</a></div>
<p>In 1856, Democrats selected Buchanan as their nominee for President of the United States. He had been in England during the <a href="/wiki/Kansas-Nebraska_Act" title="Kansas-Nebraska Act" class="mw-redirect">Kansas-Nebraska</a> debate and thus remained untainted by either side.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Pennsylvania, which had three times failed Buchanan, now gave him full support in its state convention. Though he never declared his candidacy, it is apparent from all his correspondence that he was aware of the distinct possibility of his nomination by the Democratic convention in Cincinnati, even before heading home at the finish of his work as Minister to the Court of St. James in the United Kingdom. Writer <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Hawthorne" title="Nathaniel Hawthorne">Nathaniel Hawthorne</a>, then serving as American <a href="/wiki/Consul" title="Consul">Consul</a> in Liverpool, recorded in his diary that Buchanan visited him in January 1855:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>He returns to America, he says, next October, and then retires forever from public life... as regards his prospects for the Presidency, [h]e said that his mind was fully made up, and that he would never be a candidate, and that he had expressed this decision to his friends in such a way as to put it out of his own power to change it... that it was now too late, and that he was too old... although, really, he is the only Democrat, at this moment, whom it would not be absurd to talk of for the office.... I wonder whether he can have had any object in saying all this to me. He might see that it would be perfectly natural for me to tell it to General Pierce.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jonathan_M._Foltz" title="Jonathan M. Foltz">Jonathan Foltz</a> told Buchanan in November 1855, "The people have taken the next presidency out of the hands of the politicians...the people and not your political friends will place you there." While Buchanan did not overtly seek the office, he most deliberately chose not to discourage the movement on his behalf, something that was well within his power on many occasions.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DemocraticPlatform1856Cartoon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/DemocraticPlatform1856Cartoon.jpg/175px-DemocraticPlatform1856Cartoon.jpg" width="175" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/DemocraticPlatform1856Cartoon.jpg/263px-DemocraticPlatform1856Cartoon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/DemocraticPlatform1856Cartoon.jpg/350px-DemocraticPlatform1856Cartoon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1264" data-file-height="815" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DemocraticPlatform1856Cartoon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An anti-Buchanan political cartoon from the 1856 election</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Former president <a href="/wiki/Millard_Fillmore" title="Millard Fillmore">Millard Fillmore</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Know-Nothing" title="Know-Nothing" class="mw-redirect">Know-Nothing</a>" candidacy helped Buchanan defeat <a href="/wiki/John_C._Fr%C3%A9mont" title="John C. Frémont">John C. Frémont</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/U.S._Republican_Party" title="U.S. Republican Party" class="mw-redirect">Republican</a> candidate for president in 1856. He served as president from March 4, 1857, to March 4, 1861. Buchanan remains the most recent of the two Democrats (the other being <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a>) to succeed a fellow Democrat to the Presidency by election in his own right. <a href="/wiki/President-elect_of_the_United_States" title="President-elect of the United States">President-elect</a> Buchanan stated about the growing schism in the country: "The object of my administration will be to destroy sectional party, North or South, and to restore harmony to the Union under a national and conservative government."<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> He set about this initially by maintaining a sectional balance in his appointments and persuading the people to accept constitutional law as the Supreme Court interpreted it. The court was considering the legality of restricting slavery in the territories and two justices had hinted to Buchanan their findings.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Buchanan was the last president born in the 18th century and, at age 65, was the second-oldest man to be elected President at that time.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_.281857.E2.80.931861.29">Presidency (1857–1861)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Presidency (1857–1861)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Buchanan_inauguration_1857.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/James_Buchanan_inauguration_1857.jpg/220px-James_Buchanan_inauguration_1857.jpg" width="220" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/James_Buchanan_inauguration_1857.jpg/330px-James_Buchanan_inauguration_1857.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/James_Buchanan_inauguration_1857.jpg/440px-James_Buchanan_inauguration_1857.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4030" data-file-height="3487" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Buchanan_inauguration_1857.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Inauguration of James Buchanan, March 4, 1857, from a photograph by <a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Wood_(photographer)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="John Wood (photographer) (page does not exist)">John Wood</a>: Buchanan's <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_inauguration" title="United States presidential inauguration">inauguration</a> was the first one to be recorded in photographs.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dred_Scott_case">Dred Scott case</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Dred Scott case">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In his inaugural address, besides promising not to run again, Buchanan referred to the territorial question as "happily, a matter of but little practical importance" since the Supreme Court was about to settle it "speedily and finally", and proclaimed that when the decision came, he would "cheerfully submit, whatever this may be". Two days later, <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/Roger_B._Taney" title="Roger B. Taney">Roger B. Taney</a> delivered the <a href="/wiki/Dred_Scott_v._Sandford" title="Dred Scott v. Sandford">Dred Scott decision</a>, asserting that Congress had no <a href="/wiki/U.S._Constitution" title="U.S. Constitution" class="mw-redirect">constitutional</a> power to exclude slavery in the territories. Such comments delighted Southerners and incited anger in the North.<sup id="cite_ref-Klein_p_316_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klein_p_316-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Buchanan preferred to see the territorial question resolved by the Supreme Court. He wrote to Justice <a href="/wiki/John_Catron" title="John Catron">John Catron</a> in January 1857, inquiring about the outcome of the case and suggesting that a broader decision would be more prudent.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> Catron, who was from <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, replied on February 10 that the Supreme Court's southern majority would decide against Scott, but would likely have to publish the decision on narrow grounds if there was no support from the Court's northern justices—unless Buchanan could convince his fellow Pennsylvanian, Justice <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cooper_Grier" title="Robert Cooper Grier">Robert Cooper Grier</a>, to join the majority.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> Buchanan then wrote to Grier and successfully prevailed upon him, allowing the majority leverage to issue a broad-ranging decision that transcended the specific circumstances of Scott's case to declare the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise_of_1820" title="Missouri Compromise of 1820" class="mw-redirect">Missouri Compromise of 1820</a> unconstitutional.<sup id="cite_ref-isbn0-13-195130-0_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-13-195130-0-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baker_2004.2C_p._85_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker_2004.2C_p._85-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> The correspondence was not public at the time; however, at his inauguration, Buchanan was seen in whispered conversation with Chief Justice Roger B. Taney. When the decision was issued two days later, Republicans began spreading word that Taney had revealed to Buchanan the forthcoming result.<sup id="cite_ref-Baker_2004.2C_p._85_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker_2004.2C_p._85-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>, in his 1858 <a href="/wiki/Lincoln%27s_House_Divided_Speech" title="Lincoln's House Divided Speech">House Divided Speech</a>, denounced Buchanan, Taney, Stephen A. Douglas and Franklin Pierce as accomplices of the <a href="/wiki/Slave_Power" title="Slave Power">Slave Power</a>, a supposed <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchy</a> aiming to eliminate legal barriers to slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chaos_in_Kansas.3B_Buchanan_breaks_with_Stephen_Douglas">Chaos in Kansas; Buchanan breaks with Stephen Douglas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Chaos in Kansas; Buchanan breaks with Stephen Douglas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="262" data-file-height="204" /></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(May 2013)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kansas-Nebraska_Act" title="Kansas-Nebraska Act" class="mw-redirect">Kansas-Nebraska Act</a> of 1854 created <a href="/wiki/Kansas_Territory" title="Kansas Territory">Kansas Territory</a>, and allowed the settlers there to choose whether to allow slavery. This resulted in violence between "<a href="/wiki/Free-Soil" title="Free-Soil" class="mw-redirect">Free-Soil</a>" (antislavery) and proslavery settlers in what became known as the "<a href="/wiki/Bleeding_Kansas" title="Bleeding Kansas">Bleeding Kansas</a>" crisis.</p>
<p>The antislavery settlers organized a government in <a href="/wiki/Topeka" title="Topeka" class="mw-redirect">Topeka</a>, while proslavery settlers established a seat of government in <a href="/wiki/Lecompton,_Kansas" title="Lecompton, Kansas">Lecompton, Kansas</a>. For Kansas to be admitted to statehood, a state constitution had to be submitted to Congress with the approval of a majority of residents.</p>
<p>Toward this end, Buchanan appointed <a href="/wiki/Robert_J._Walker" title="Robert J. Walker">Robert J. Walker</a> as territorial governor, with the mission of reconciling the settler factions and approving a constitution. Walker, who was from <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, was expected to assist the proslavery faction in gaining approval of their <a href="/wiki/Lecompton_Constitution" title="Lecompton Constitution">Lecompton Constitution</a>. However, most Kansas settlers were Free-Soilers. The Lecomptonites held a referendum, which Free-Soilers boycotted, with trick terms and claimed their constitution was adopted. Walker resigned in disgust.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Nevertheless, Buchanan now pushed for congressional approval of Kansas statehood under the Lecompton Constitution. Buchanan made every effort to secure congressional approval, offering favors, patronage appointments, and even cash for votes. The Lecompton bill passed through the House of Representatives, but failed in the Senate, where it was opposed by Senator <a href="/wiki/Stephen_A._Douglas" title="Stephen A. Douglas">Stephen A. Douglas</a> of <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, leader of the northern Democrats. Douglas advocated "popular sovereignty" (letting settlers decide on slavery—nicknamed "<a href="/wiki/Popular_sovereignty_in_the_United_States" title="Popular sovereignty in the United States">squatter sovereignty</a>" by Douglas' opponents); he rejected the fraudulent way the Lecompton Constitution was supposedly adopted.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The battle over Kansas escalated into a battle for control of the Democratic Party. On one side were Buchanan, most Southern Democrats, and northern Democrats allied to the Southerners ("Doughfaces"); on the other side were Douglas and most northern Democrats plus a few Southerners. The struggle lasted from 1857 to 1860. Buchanan used his patronage powers to remove Douglas' sympathizers in Illinois and Washington, DC and installed proadministration Democrats, including postmasters.<sup id="cite_ref-Buchanan_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buchanan-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Douglas's Senate term ended in 1859, so the Illinois legislature elected in 1858 had to choose whether or not to re-elect him. The Senate choice was the primary issue of the legislative election, marked by the famous <a href="/wiki/Lincoln-Douglas_debates" title="Lincoln-Douglas debates" class="mw-redirect">Lincoln-Douglas debates</a>. Buchanan, working through Federal patronage appointees in Illinois, ran candidates for the legislature in competition with both the Republicans and the Douglas Democrats. This could easily have thrown the election to the Republicans—which showed the depth of Buchanan's animosity toward Douglas.<sup id="cite_ref-Chadwick_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chadwick-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the end, however, the Danite vote was insignificant. Douglas Democrats won the legislative election and Douglas was re-elected to the Senate. Douglas forces took control throughout the North, except in Buchanan's home state of Pennsylvania. Buchanan was reduced to a narrow base of southern supporters.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Buchanan.27s_political_views">Buchanan's political views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Buchanan's political views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_James_Buchanan_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_James_Buchanan_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_James_Buchanan_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_James_Buchanan_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_James_Buchanan_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_James_Buchanan_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_James_Buchanan_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4828" data-file-height="6354" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_James_Buchanan_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
James Buchanan (1859) by <a href="/wiki/George_Healy" title="George Healy" class="mw-redirect">George Healy</a> as seen in the <a href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery_(United_States)" title="National Portrait Gallery (United States)">National Portrait Gallery</a> in Washington, DC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Buchanan considered the essence of good self-government to be founded on restraint. The constitution he considered to be "...restraints, imposed not by arbitrary authority, but by the people upon themselves and their representatives.... In an enlarged view, the people's interests may seem identical, but "to the eye of local and sectional prejudice, they always appear to be conflicting ... and the jealousies that will perpetually arise can be repressed only by the mutual forbearance which pervades the constitution."<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>One of the greatest issues of the day was tariffs. Buchanan condemned both free trade and prohibitive tariffs, since either would benefit one section of the country to the detriment of the other. As the senator from Pennsylvania, he thought: "I am viewed as the strongest advocate of protection in other states, whilst I am denounced as its enemy in Pennsylvania."<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Buchanan, like many of his time, was torn between his desire to expand the country for the benefit of all and his insistence on guaranteeing to the people settling the expanded areas their rights, including slavery. On territorial expansion, he said, "What, sir? Prevent the people from crossing the Rocky Mountains? You might just as well command the Niagara not to flow. We must fulfill our destiny."<sup id="cite_ref-klein147_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klein147-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> On the resulting spread of slavery, through unconditional expansion, he stated: "I feel a strong repugnance by any act of mine to extend the present limits of the Union over a new slave-holding territory." For instance, he hoped the acquisition of Texas would "be the means of limiting, not enlarging, the dominion of slavery".<sup id="cite_ref-klein147_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klein147-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Nevertheless, in deference to the intentions of the typical slaveholder, he was quick to provide the benefit of much doubt. In his third annual message, Buchanan claimed that the slaves were "treated with kindness and humanity.... Both the philanthropy and the self-interest of the master have combined to produce this humane result."<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Historian Kenneth Stampp wrote: "Shortly after his election, he assured a southern Senator that the "great object" of his administration would be "to arrest, if possible, the agitation of the Slavery question in the North and to destroy sectional parties. Should a kind <a href="/wiki/Divine_Providence" title="Divine Providence" class="mw-redirect">Providence</a> enable me to succeed in my efforts to restore harmony to the Union, I shall feel that I have not lived in vain." In the northern anti-slavery idiom of his day, Buchanan was often considered a "doughface", a northern man with southern principles.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The President, however, also felt that "this question of domestic slavery is the weak point in our institutions, touch this question seriously ... and the Union is from that moment dissolved. Although in Pennsylvania we are all opposed to slavery in the abstract, we can never violate the constitutional compact we have with our sister states. Their rights will be held sacred by us. Under the constitution it is their own question; and there let it remain."<sup id="cite_ref-Klein150_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klein150-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Buchanan was irked that the abolitionists, in his view, were preventing the solution to the slavery problem. He stated, "Before [the abolitionists] commenced this agitation, a very large and growing party existed in several of the slave states in favor of the gradual abolition of slavery; and now not a voice is heard there in support of such a measure. The abolitionists have postponed the emancipation of the slaves in three or four states for at least half a century."<sup id="cite_ref-Klein150_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klein150-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Buchanan greatly valued education, but believed that colleges were the duty of state governments rather than the central government, as expressed in his veto of a bill to grant land for colleges.</p>
<p>"It is extremely doubtful, to say the least, whether this bill would contribute to the advancement of agriculture and the mechanic arts--objects the dignity and value of which can not be too highly appreciated."</p>
<p>"The Federal Government, which makes the donation, has confessedly no constitutional power to follow it into the States and enforce the application of the fund to the intended objects. As donors we shall possess no control over our own gift after it shall have passed from our hands. It is true that the State legislatures are required to stipulate that they will faithfully execute the trust in the manner prescribed by the bill. But should they fail to do this, what would be the consequence? The Federal Government has no power, and ought to have no power, to compel the execution of the trust."</p>
<p>Near the end of his administration, he had a serious exchange with the Rev. William Paxton. After what Paxton described as quite a probative discussion, Buchanan said, " Well, sir ... I hope I am a Christian. I have much of the experience you have described, and as soon as I retire, I will unite with the Presbyterian Church." Paxton asked why he delayed, to which he replied, "I must delay for the honor of religion. If I were to unite with the church now, they would say 'hypocrite' from Maine to Georgia."<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Panic_of_1857">Panic of 1857</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Panic of 1857">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1857" title="Panic of 1857">Panic of 1857</a> began in summer of that year, brought on mostly by the people's overconsumption of goods from Europe to such an extent that the Union's <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">specie</a> was drained off, overbuilding by competing railroads, and rampant land speculation in the west. Most of the state banks had overextended credit, to more than $7.00 for each dollar of gold or silver. The Republicans considered the Congress to be the culprit for having recently reduced tariffs.</p>
<p>Buchanan's response, outlined in his first Annual Message to Congress, was "reform not relief". While the government was "without the power to extend relief",<sup id="cite_ref-Baker.2C_p._90_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker.2C_p._90-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> it would continue to pay its debts in specie, and while it would not curtail public works, none would be added. He urged the states to restrict the banks to a credit level of $3 to $1 of specie, and discouraged the use of federal or state bonds as security for bank note issues. The economy did eventually recover, though many Americans suffered as a result of the panic.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> The South, due to an agriculture-based economy, was considered to have been less severely affected than the North, where manufacturers were hardest hit. Buchanan, by the time he left office in 1861, had accumulated a federal deficit of $17&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-Baker.2C_p._90_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker.2C_p._90-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Utah_War">Utah War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Utah War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Utah_War" title="Utah War">Utah War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Runaway_Officials_of_1851" title="Runaway Officials of 1851">Runaway Officials of 1851</a></div>
<p>In March 1857, Buchanan received conflicting reports from federal judges in the <a href="/wiki/Utah_Territory" title="Utah Territory">Utah Territory</a> that their offices had been disrupted and they had been driven from their posts by the <a href="/wiki/Mormons" title="Mormons">Mormons</a>. He knew that the Pierce administration had refused to facilitate Utah being granted statehood and the Mormons feared the loss of their property rights. Accepting the wildest rumors and believing the Mormons to be in open rebellion against the United States, Buchanan sent the Army in November of that year to replace <a href="/wiki/Brigham_Young" title="Brigham Young">Brigham Young</a> as governor with the non-Mormon <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Cumming_(governor)" title="Alfred Cumming (governor)">Alfred Cumming</a>. While the Mormons' defiance of federal authority in the past had become traditional, some question whether Buchanan's action was a justifiable or prudent response to uncorroborated reports.<sup id="cite_ref-Klein_p_316_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klein_p_316-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> Complicating matters, Young's notice of his replacement was not delivered because the Pierce administration had annulled the Utah mail contract.<sup id="cite_ref-Klein_p_316_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klein_p_316-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> After Young reacted to the military action by mustering a two-week expedition destroying wagon trains, oxen, and other Army property, Buchanan dispatched <a href="/wiki/Thomas_L._Kane" title="Thomas L. Kane">Thomas L. Kane</a> as a private agent to negotiate peace. The mission succeeded, the new governor was shortly placed in office, and the <a href="/wiki/Utah_War" title="Utah War">Utah War</a> ended. The President granted amnesty to all inhabitants who would respect the authority of the government, and moved the federal troops to a nonthreatening distance for the balance of his administration.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Partisan_deadlock">Partisan deadlock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Partisan deadlock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The division between northern and southern Democrats allowed the Republicans to win a <a href="/wiki/Plurality_(voting)" title="Plurality (voting)">plurality</a> in the House in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_election,_1858" title="United States House election, 1858" class="mw-redirect">election of 1858</a>. Their control of the chamber allowed the Republicans to block most of Buchanan's agenda (including his proposals for expansion of influence in Central America, and for the purchase of Cuba). Buchanan thought the ideologies of the United States would bring peace and prosperity to these neighboring lands as they had in the Northwest and that without U.S. influence, the major European powers would intervene. The imperative of safe and speedy travel from east to west was of strategic importance to the country. These goals would not be reached. Buchanan, in turn, vetoed six substantial pieces of Republican legislation, causing further hostility between Congress and the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Covode_committee">Covode committee</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Covode committee">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In March 1860, the House created the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Select_Committee_to_Investigate_Alleged_Corruptions_in_Government" title="United States House Select Committee to Investigate Alleged Corruptions in Government">Covode committee</a> to investigate the administration for evidence of offenses, some impeachable, such as bribery and extortion of representatives in exchange for their votes. The committee, with three Republicans and two Democrats, was accused by Buchanan's supporters of being nakedly partisan; they also charged its chairman, Republican Rep. <a href="/wiki/John_Covode" title="John Covode">John Covode</a>, with acting on a personal grudge (since the president had vetoed a bill that was fashioned as a land grant for new agricultural colleges, but was designed to benefit Covode's railroad company<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup>). However, the Democratic committee members, as well as Democratic witnesses, were equally enthusiastic in their pursuit of Buchanan, and as pointed in their condemnations, as the Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Grossman_2003_78_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grossman_2003_78-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The committee was unable to establish grounds for impeaching Buchanan; however, the majority report issued on June 17 exposed corruption and abuse of power among members of his cabinet, as well as allegations (if not impeachable evidence) from the Republican members of the Committee, that Buchanan had attempted to bribe members of Congress in connection with the Lecompton constitution. (The Democratic report, issued separately the same day, pointed out that evidence was scarce, but did not refute the allegations; one of the Democratic members, Rep. <a href="/wiki/James_Carroll_Robinson" title="James Carroll Robinson">James Robinson</a>, stated publicly that he agreed with the Republican report even though he did not sign it.<sup id="cite_ref-Grossman_2003_78_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grossman_2003_78-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup>)</p>
<p>Buchanan claimed to have "passed triumphantly through this ordeal" with complete vindication. Nonetheless, Republican operatives distributed thousands of copies of the Covode Committee report throughout the nation as campaign material in that year's presidential election.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disintegration:_Election_of_1860">Disintegration: Election of 1860</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Disintegration: Election of 1860">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_C_Breckinridge-04775-restored.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/John_C_Breckinridge-04775-restored.jpg/200px-John_C_Breckinridge-04775-restored.jpg" width="200" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/John_C_Breckinridge-04775-restored.jpg/300px-John_C_Breckinridge-04775-restored.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/John_C_Breckinridge-04775-restored.jpg/400px-John_C_Breckinridge-04775-restored.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2050" data-file-height="2755" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_C_Breckinridge-04775-restored.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/John_C._Breckinridge" title="John C. Breckinridge">John C. Breckinridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President of the United States</a> under Buchanan</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sectional strife rose to such a pitch that the Democratic Party's national convention in 1860 led directly to a schism in the Party. Buchanan played little part at the national convention, meeting in <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston, South Carolina</a>. The southern wing walked out of the convention and nominated its own candidate for the presidency, incumbent Vice President <a href="/wiki/John_C._Breckinridge" title="John C. Breckinridge">John C. Breckinridge</a>. Another faction nominated former Speaker of the House <a href="/wiki/John_Bell_(Tennessee_politician)" title="John Bell (Tennessee politician)">John Bell</a>, who took no position on slavery; his only focus was on saving the Union. The remainder of the party finally nominated Buchanan's archenemy, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Douglas" title="Stephen Douglas" class="mw-redirect">Stephen Douglas</a>. For his part, President Buchanan supported Breckinridge's candidacy. When the Republicans nominated Abraham Lincoln, it was a near certainty that he would be elected.</p>
<p>As early as October, the army's <a href="/wiki/Commanding_General_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Commanding General of the United States Army">Commanding General</a>, <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a>, warned Buchanan that Lincoln's election would likely cause at least seven states to secede. He also recommended to Buchanan that massive amounts of federal troops and artillery be deployed to those states to protect federal property, although he also warned that few reinforcements were available (Congress had since 1857 failed to heed both men's calls for a stronger militia and had allowed the Army to fall into deplorable condition.<sup id="cite_ref-Klein_1962_pp._356_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klein_1962_pp._356-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup>) Buchanan, however, distrusted Scott (the two had long been political adversaries) and ignored his recommendations.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> After Lincoln's election, Buchanan directed War Secretary Floyd to reinforce southern forts with such provisions, arms and men as were available; however, Floyd convinced him to revoke the order.<sup id="cite_ref-Klein_1962_pp._356_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klein_1962_pp._356-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>With Lincoln's victory, talk of secession and disunion reached a boiling point. Buchanan was forced to address it in his final message to Congress. Both factions awaited news of how Buchanan would deal with the question. In his message,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> Buchanan denied the legal right of states to secede but held that the federal government legally could not prevent them. He placed the blame for the crisis solely on "intemperate interference of the Northern people with the question of slavery in the Southern States", and suggested that if they did not "repeal their unconstitutional and obnoxious enactments ... the injured States, after having first used all peaceful and constitutional means to obtain redress, would be justified in revolutionary resistance to the Government of the Union."<sup id="cite_ref-1860message_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1860message-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Buchanan's only suggestion to solve the crisis was "an explanatory amendment" reaffirming the constitutionality of slavery in the states, the fugitive slave laws, and <a href="/wiki/Popular_sovereignty" title="Popular sovereignty">popular sovereignty</a> in the territories.<sup id="cite_ref-1860message_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1860message-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> His address was sharply criticized both by the north, for its refusal to stop secession, and the south, for denying its right to secede.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Five days after the address was delivered, Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/Howell_Cobb" title="Howell Cobb">Howell Cobb</a> resigned, feeling that his views and the President's had become irreconcilable.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buchanan_as_Dog_Buck_1861.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Buchanan_as_Dog_Buck_1861.tif/lossy-page1-300px-Buchanan_as_Dog_Buck_1861.tif.jpg" width="300" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Buchanan_as_Dog_Buck_1861.tif/lossy-page1-450px-Buchanan_as_Dog_Buck_1861.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Buchanan_as_Dog_Buck_1861.tif/lossy-page1-600px-Buchanan_as_Dog_Buck_1861.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1056" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buchanan_as_Dog_Buck_1861.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Columbia_(name)" title="Columbia (name)">Columbia</a> as <a href="/wiki/Little_Bo_Peep" title="Little Bo Peep">Little Bo Peep</a>; her lost sheep are the Southern states. Buchanan as "dog buck" tries in vain to herd states back into the Union.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Efforts were made by statesmen such as Sen. <a href="/wiki/John_J._Crittenden" title="John J. Crittenden">John J. Crittenden</a>, Rep. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Corwin" title="Thomas Corwin">Thomas Corwin</a>, and former president <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">John Tyler</a> to negotiate a compromise to stop secession, with Buchanan's support; all failed. Failed efforts to compromise were also made by a group of governors meeting in New York. Buchanan employed a last-minute tactic, in secret, to bring a solution. He attempted in vain to procure President-elect Lincoln's call for a constitutional convention or national referendum to resolve the issue of slavery. Lincoln declined.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>South Carolina declared its secession on December 20, 1860, followed by six other <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">slave states</a>, and, by February 1861, they had formed the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate States of America</a>. As Scott had surmised, the secessionist governments declared eminent domain over federal property within their states; Buchanan and his administration took no action to stop the confiscation of government property.</p>
<p>Beginning in late December, Buchanan reorganized his cabinet, ousting Confederate sympathizers and replacing them with hard-line <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalists</a> <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah_S._Black" title="Jeremiah S. Black">Jeremiah S. Black</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edwin_M._Stanton" title="Edwin M. Stanton" class="mw-redirect">Edwin M. Stanton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Holt" title="Joseph Holt">Joseph Holt</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Adams_Dix" title="John Adams Dix">John A. Dix</a>. These conservative Democrats strongly believed in American nationalism and refused to countenance secession. At one point, Treasury Secretary Dix ordered Treasury agents in New Orleans, "If any man pulls down the <a href="/wiki/American_flag" title="American flag" class="mw-redirect">American flag</a>, shoot him on the spot." The new cabinet advised Buchanan to request from Congress the authority to call up militias and give himself emergency military powers, and this he did, on January 8, 1861. Nevertheless, by that time Buchanan's relations with Congress were so strained that his requests were rejected out of hand. Many consider him as the worst President in history for his failure to prevent the American Civil War.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fort_Sumter">Fort Sumter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Fort Sumter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Before Buchanan left office, all arsenals and forts in the seceding states were lost (except <a href="/wiki/Fort_Sumter" title="Fort Sumter">Fort Sumter</a>, off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston, South Carolina</a>, and three island outposts in Florida), and a fourth of all federal soldiers surrendered to the Texas militia. Knowing that secessionist fervor was strongest in <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>, Buchanan made a quiet pact with South Carolina's legislators that he would not reinforce the Charleston garrison in exchange for no interference from the state.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> However, Buchanan did not inform the Charleston commander, <a href="/wiki/Major_Robert_Anderson" title="Major Robert Anderson" class="mw-redirect">Major Robert Anderson</a>, of the agreement, and on December 26 Anderson violated it by moving his command to Fort Sumter. Southerners responded with a demand that Buchanan remove Anderson, while northerners demanded support for the commander. On December 31, in an apparent panic and without consulting Anderson, Buchanan ordered reinforcements.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On January 5, Buchanan sent civilian steamer <i><a href="/wiki/Star_of_the_West" title="Star of the West">Star of the West</a></i> to carry reinforcements and supplies to Fort Sumter, which, located in Charleston harbor, was a conspicuously visible spot in the Confederacy. On January 9, 1861, South Carolina state batteries opened fire on the ship, causing it to withdraw and return to New York. Buchanan was again criticized by both north (for lack of retaliation against the hostile South Carolina batteries) and south (for attempting to reinforce Fort Sumter), further alienating both factions.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-presidentprofiles_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presidentprofiles-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Paralyzed, Buchanan made no further moves either to prepare for war or to avert it.</p>
<p>On Buchanan's final day as president, March 4, 1861, he remarked to the incoming Lincoln, "If you are as happy in entering the White House as I shall feel on returning to <a href="/wiki/Wheatland_(Lancaster)" title="Wheatland (Lancaster)" class="mw-redirect">Wheatland</a>, you are a happy man."<sup id="cite_ref-Baker_140_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker_140-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
