<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Judicial appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_James_Buchanan" title="List of federal judges appointed by James Buchanan">List of federal judges appointed by James Buchanan</a></div>
<p>Buchanan appointed one Justice to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Clifford" title="Nathan Clifford">Nathan Clifford</a>. Buchanan appointed only seven other Article III federal judges, all to <a href="/wiki/United_States_district_court" title="United States district court">United States district courts</a>. He also appointed two Article I judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Claims" title="United States Court of Claims">United States Court of Claims</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States_admitted_to_the_Union">States admitted to the Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: States admitted to the Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>&#160;– May 11, 1858</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>&#160;– February 14, 1859</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>&#160;– January 29, 1861</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years">Final years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Final years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Buchanan_-_post_presidency.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/James_Buchanan_-_post_presidency.jpg/200px-James_Buchanan_-_post_presidency.jpg" width="200" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/James_Buchanan_-_post_presidency.jpg/300px-James_Buchanan_-_post_presidency.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/James_Buchanan_-_post_presidency.jpg/400px-James_Buchanan_-_post_presidency.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2046" data-file-height="2640" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Buchanan_-_post_presidency.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Buchanan in his later years</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Civil War erupted within two months of Buchanan's retirement. He supported it, writing to former colleagues that "the assault upon Sumter was the commencement of war by the Confederate states, and no alternative was left but to prosecute it with vigor on our part".<sup id="cite_ref-Birkner_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birkner-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> He also wrote a letter to his fellow Pennsylvania Democrats, urging them to "join the many thousands of brave &amp; patriotic volunteers who are already in the field".<sup id="cite_ref-Birkner_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birkner-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>However, Buchanan spent most of his remaining years defending himself from public blame for the Civil War, which was even referred to by some as "Buchanan's War".<sup id="cite_ref-Birkner_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birkner-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> He began receiving angry and threatening letters daily, and stores displayed Buchanan's likeness with the eyes inked red, a noose drawn around his neck and the word "TRAITOR" written across his forehead. The Senate proposed a resolution of condemnation which ultimately failed, and newspapers accused him of colluding with the Confederacy. His former cabinet members, five of whom had been given jobs in the Lincoln administration, refused to defend Buchanan publicly.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Initially so disturbed by the attacks that he fell ill and depressed, Buchanan finally began defending himself in October 1862, in an exchange of letters between himself and <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a> that was published in the <i><a href="/wiki/National_Intelligencer" title="National Intelligencer">National Intelligencer</a></i> newspaper.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> He soon began writing his fullest public defense, in the form of his memoir <i>Mr. Buchanan's Administration on the Eve of Rebellion</i>, which was published in 1866.</p>
<p>Buchanan caught a cold in May 1868, which quickly worsened due to his advanced age. He died on June 1, 1868, from <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_failure" title="Respiratory failure">respiratory failure</a> at the age of 77 at his home at <a href="/wiki/Wheatland_(Lancaster)" title="Wheatland (Lancaster)" class="mw-redirect">Wheatland</a> and was interred in <a href="/wiki/Woodward_Hill_Cemetery" title="Woodward Hill Cemetery">Woodward Hill Cemetery</a> in Lancaster.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Rufus_King..jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/William_Rufus_King..jpg/220px-William_Rufus_King..jpg" width="220" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/William_Rufus_King..jpg/330px-William_Rufus_King..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/William_Rufus_King..jpg 2x" data-file-width="352" data-file-height="461" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Rufus_King..jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/William_R._King" title="William R. King">William Rufus DeVane King</a>, 13th <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President of the United States</a>, a friend of James Buchanan, with whom he shared a Washington boardinghouse.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1818, Buchanan met Anne Caroline Coleman at a grand ball at Lancaster's White Swan Inn, and the two began courting. Anne was the daughter of the wealthy iron manufacturing businessman (and protective father) Robert Coleman and sister-in-law of Philadelphia judge <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Hemphill" title="Joseph Hemphill">Joseph Hemphill</a>, one of Buchanan's colleagues from the House of Representatives. By 1819, the two were engaged, but could spend little time together; Buchanan was extremely busy with his law firm and political projects during the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1819" title="Panic of 1819">Panic of 1819</a>, which took him away from Coleman for weeks at a time. Conflicting rumors abounded, suggesting that he was marrying her for her money, because his own family was less affluent, or that he was involved with other women. Buchanan never publicly spoke of his motives or feelings, but letters from Anne revealed she was paying heed to the rumors.</p>
<p>After Buchanan visited a friend's wife, Coleman broke off the engagement. She died suddenly soon afterward, on December 9, 1819. The records of a Dr. Chapman, who looked after her in her final hours, and who commented just after her death that it was "the first instance he ever knew of <a href="/wiki/Female_hysteria" title="Female hysteria">hysteria</a> producing death", reveal that he theorized, despite the absence of any valid evidence, that she had overdosed on <a href="/wiki/Laudanum" title="Laudanum">laudanum</a>, a concentrated tincture of opium.<sup id="cite_ref-AH_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AH-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Buchanan was prevented from attending the funeral service. In a letter to her father, he wrote that "I feel happiness has fled from me forever."<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After Coleman's death, Buchanan never courted another woman or seemed to show any emotional or physical interest; a rumor circulated of an affair with President <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a>'s widow, <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Childress_Polk" title="Sarah Childress Polk">Sarah Childress Polk</a>, but it had no basis.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> It has been suggested that Anne's death in fact served to deflect awkward questions about his sexuality and bachelorhood.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> While Buchanan may have been <a href="/wiki/Asexuality" title="Asexuality">asexual</a> or <a href="/wiki/Celibate" title="Celibate" class="mw-redirect">celibate</a>, several have put forth arguments that he was <a href="/wiki/Homosexual" title="Homosexual" class="mw-redirect">homosexual</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bisexual" title="Bisexual" class="mw-redirect">bisexual</a>: Shelley Ross, biographer Jean Baker, sociologist <a href="/wiki/James_W._Loewen" title="James W. Loewen">James W. Loewen</a>, author Robert P. Watson, and historian John Howard.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A source of this interest has been Buchanan's close and intimate relationship with <a href="/wiki/William_R._King" title="William R. King">William Rufus King</a> (who became Vice President under <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a>). The two men lived together in a Washington boardinghouse for 10 years from 1834 until King's departure for France in 1844. King referred to the relationship as a "communion",<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> and the two attended social functions together. Contemporaries also noted the closeness. Andrew Jackson called them "Miss Nancy" and "Aunt Fancy" (the former being a 19th-century euphemism for an effeminate man<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup>), while <a href="/wiki/Aaron_V._Brown" title="Aaron V. Brown">Aaron V. Brown</a> referred to King as Buchanan's "better half".<sup id="cite_ref-Baker_75_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker_75-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> James W. Loewen described Buchanan and King as "siamese twins". In later years, Kat Thompson, the wife of a cabinet member, expressed her anxiety that "there was something unhealthy in the president's attitude".<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Buchanan adopted King's mannerisms and romanticised view of southern culture. Both had strong political ambitions, and in 1844 they planned to run as president and vice president. One historian has found them both to be soft, effeminate, and eccentric.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_72-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> In May 1844, Buchanan wrote to Cornelia Roosevelt, "I am now 'solitary and alone', having no companion in the house with me. I have gone a wooing to several gentlemen, but have not succeeded with any one of them. I feel that it is not good for man to be alone, and [I] should not be astonished to find myself married to some old maid who can nurse me when I am sick, provide good dinners for me when I am well, and not expect from me any very ardent or romantic affection."<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_72-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>King became ill in 1853 and died of tuberculosis shortly after Pierce's inauguration, four years before Buchanan became President. Buchanan described him as "among the best, the purest and most consistent public men I have known."<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_72-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> The length and intimacy of their surviving letters illustrate "the affection of a special friendship."</p>
<p>During Buchanan's presidency, his orphaned niece, <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Lane" title="Harriet Lane">Harriet Lane</a>, whom he had adopted, served as official White House hostess.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Buchanan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="width: 230px; height: 270px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -45px; left: -50px; width: 325px"><a href="/wiki/File:BUCHANAN,_James-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Buchanan as President." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/BUCHANAN%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/325px-BUCHANAN%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="325" height="403" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/BUCHANAN%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/488px-BUCHANAN%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/BUCHANAN%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/650px-BUCHANAN%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="9000" data-file-height="11168" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BUCHANAN,_James-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:BUCHANAN, James-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:BUCHANAN, James-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Buchanan as President.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The day before his own death, Buchanan predicted that "history will vindicate my memory".<sup id="cite_ref-state_park_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state_park-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, historians criticize Buchanan for his unwillingness or inability to act in the face of secession. <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_United_States_Presidents" title="Historical rankings of United States Presidents" class="mw-redirect">Historical rankings of United States Presidents</a>, considering presidential achievements, leadership qualities, failures and faults, consistently place Buchanan among the least successful presidents.<sup id="cite_ref-us_news_poll_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-us_news_poll-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-times_poll_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-times_poll-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> In an academic poll of 47 British academics specializing in American history and politics in 2011, it was reported that he came last (40th). They were asked to evaluate the performance of every president from 1789 to 2009 (excluding <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Harrison" title="William Henry Harrison">William Henry Harrison</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Garfield" title="James Garfield" class="mw-redirect">James Garfield</a>, both of whom died shortly after taking office) in five categories: vision/agenda-setting, domestic leadership, foreign policy leadership, moral authority and positive historical significance of their legacy.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Buchanan biographer Philip Klein put these rankings into context commenting, "Buchanan assumed leadership ... when an unprecedented wave of angry passion was sweeping over the nation. That he held the hostile sections in check during these revolutionary times was in itself a remarkable achievement. His weaknesses in the stormy years of his presidency were magnified by enraged partisans of the North and South. His many talents, which in a quieter era might have gained for him a place among the great presidents, were quickly overshadowed by the cataclysmic events of civil war and by the towering <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Others criticized Buchanan as well. Years before Buchanan won the White House, President <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a> confided to his diary: "Mr. Buchanan is an able man, but is in small matter without judgment and sometimes acts like an old maid."<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> The <i>National Intelligencer,</i> the leading Whig newspaper of the day, ridiculed Buchanan on January 24, 1859, for his follies as president, citing a series of his "magnificent" proposals that all failed:</p>
<dl>
<dd>We must retrench the extravagant list of magnificent schemes which received the sanction of the Executive ... the great Napoleon himself, with all the resources of an empire at his sole command, never ventured the simultaneous accomplishments of so many daring projects. The acquisition of Cuba ...&#160;; the construction of a Pacific Railroad ...&#160;; a Mexican protectorate, the international preponderance in Central America, in spite of all the powers of Europe; the submission of distant South American states; ... the enlargement of the Navy; a largely increased standing Army ... what government on earth could possibly meet all the exigencies of such a flood of innovations?<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Buchanan is remembered today for other missed opportunities rather than any significant accomplishments. He vetoed the <a href="/wiki/Morrill_Act" title="Morrill Act" class="mw-redirect">Morrill Act</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Homestead_Act" title="Homestead Act" class="mw-redirect">Homestead Act</a>, both of which Lincoln signed into law only a few years later; the Homestead Act accelerated westward expansion, while the Morrill Act accelerated agricultural and engineering research and education to develop the young nation.</p>
<p>A bronze and granite memorial residing near the southeast corner of Washington, D.C.'s <a href="/wiki/Meridian_Hill_Park" title="Meridian Hill Park">Meridian Hill Park</a> was designed by architect William Gorden Beecher and sculpted by Maryland artist <a href="/wiki/Hans_Schuler" title="Hans Schuler">Hans Schuler</a>. Commissioned in 1916 but not approved by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Congress" title="U.S. Congress" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Congress</a> until 1918, and not completed and unveiled until June 26, 1930, the memorial features a statue of Buchanan bookended by male and female classical figures representing law and diplomacy, with the engraved text reading: "The incorruptible statesman whose walk was upon the mountain ranges of the law", a quote from a member of Buchanan's cabinet, <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah_S._Black" title="Jeremiah S. Black">Jeremiah S. Black</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BuchananmonmtDC.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/BuchananmonmtDC.JPG/250px-BuchananmonmtDC.JPG" width="250" height="79" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/BuchananmonmtDC.JPG/375px-BuchananmonmtDC.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/BuchananmonmtDC.JPG/500px-BuchananmonmtDC.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3138" data-file-height="992" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BuchananmonmtDC.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Buchanan memorial, Washington, D.C.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The memorial in the nation's capital complemented an earlier monument, constructed in 1907–08 and dedicated in 1911, on the site of Buchanan's birthplace in <a href="/wiki/Buchanan%27s_Birthplace_State_Park" title="Buchanan's Birthplace State Park">Stony Batter, Pennsylvania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> Part of the original 18.5-acre (75,000&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) memorial site, is a 250-ton pyramid structure which stands on the site of the original cabin where Buchanan was born. The monument was designed to show the original weathered surface of the native rubble and mortar.</p>
<p>Three counties are named in his honor: <a href="/wiki/Buchanan_County,_Iowa" title="Buchanan County, Iowa">Buchanan County, Iowa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buchanan_County,_Missouri" title="Buchanan County, Missouri">Buchanan County, Missouri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Buchanan_County,_Virginia" title="Buchanan County, Virginia">Buchanan County, Virginia</a>. Another in Texas was christened in 1858 but renamed <a href="/wiki/Stephens_County,_Texas" title="Stephens County, Texas">Stephens County</a>, after the newly elected Vice President of the Confederate States of America, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Stephens" title="Alexander Stephens" class="mw-redirect">Alexander Stephens</a>, in 1861.<sup id="cite_ref-texas_county_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-texas_county-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
